# FutureMUD Medieval Household Goods, Furniture, Light Sources, Doors, Locks and Containers Seeder Design Reference

This document provides the completed medieval-period design reference and item catalogue for a portable household-goods seeder catalogue. It covers storage containers, personal containers, furniture containers, religious containers and furnishings, food service, tableware, household vessels, doors and gates, locks, latches, keys, lock hardware, furniture, light and heat sources, water sources and washing fixtures, and decorative household wares for a broad 500AD-1300AD setting.

## Executive summary

- Total item prototypes in this reference: **1751**.
- The catalogue uses shared medieval prototypes for common domestic functions, plus regional additions where material, form, social use, or household practice meaningfully changes the item.
- All items are finished goods, skinnable, player-visible, and authored as moveable portable inventory objects unless a separate implementation deliberately creates installed room-fixture variants.
- Public item fields should remain in-world and form-based. Religious object names are acceptable where they name the visible furnishing, container, vessel, or architectural form; broad historical and religious-family labels remain builder-facing organization aids.
- The catalogue favours behaviour-stable base prototypes. Skins can carry local fantasy names, marks, paint, carving, textile patterning, fittings, status variants, and household motifs without duplicating component-heavy base items.
- Outfit manifests are not used for this reference.

## Scope and era model

- Chronological band: 500AD to 1300AD.
- Geographic coverage: Britain and Ireland; Scandinavia and the North Sea; western, central, and southern Europe; Iberia; Byzantium; the Levant; Egypt and North Africa; the Eurasian steppe; Rus/Novgorod; northern and southern India; China; Korea; and Japan.
- Historical inspiration families are used for design coverage only. Public item names, short descriptions, and full descriptions should describe visible form, material, construction, and use rather than attaching modern academic labels to the object.
- Resolution: standard household prototypes rather than exhaustive local typology. The base items should stand alone credibly while skins and later focused catalogues handle finer local decoration and world-specific naming.

### Culture coverage table

| Inspiration family | Reference anchor | Coverage boundary | Household-design focus |
|---|---:|---|---|
| Early Anglo-Saxon | c. 800AD | lowland England before sustained Anglo-Danish synthesis | simple timber chests, ash and oak storage, domestic pouches, hanging shelves, board doors, benches, stools, rush and wool household textiles |
| Anglo-Danish | c. 950AD | late Anglo-Saxon England with strong Scandinavian settlement influence | North-Sea chests, mixed timber and bronze-fitted storage, travel packs, domestic shelves, board doors, iron locks, and practical textile wares |
| Norse / Viking Age | c. 950AD | Scandinavia and diaspora settlements before the close of the Viking Age | pine and birch chests, ship-carved boxes, travelling packs, quivers, heavy benches, lamps, simple locks, and portable household storage |
| Norman | c. 1066AD | ducal Normandy and conquest-generation Norman sphere | arched cupboards, walnut or oak chests, lockboxes, sideboards, plank doors, stone and ceramic domestic vessels, and sturdier elite furniture |
| Anglo-Norman | c. 1150AD | post-Conquest England and Norman-influenced elite fashion in Britain | panelled storage, chamber chests, writing desks, hutch furniture, iron keys, wardrobes, and feast-serving wares |
| High English / British | c. 1250AD | high medieval England and Welsh March/British court-facing styles | joined furniture, moulded storage, wardrobes, drawers, sideboards, lockable chests, brass or iron fittings, and urban domestic lighting |
| Irish / Gaelic | c. 1100AD | Gaelic Ireland before strong late-medieval English tailoring influence | oak chests, hide-bound storage, linen sacks, simple shelves, travel packs, domestic vessels, low benches, and practical hearth goods |
| Scottish / Gaelic-Lowland | c. 1250AD | medieval Scotland across Gaelic Highland and Lowland court-facing habits | rugged chests, winter bedding boxes, wool and leather packs, shelves, timber doors, iron latches, and warm domestic textiles |
| Carolingian / Frankish | c. 800AD | Frankish court and common household practice under Carolingian influence | plain timber storage, tablet-banded fine pieces, domestic chests, low tables, benches, lamps, simple keys, and portable containers |
| Capetian French | c. 1200AD | northern and central French high medieval household styles | carved cupboards, fine walnut furniture, lockboxes, sideboards, domestic vessels, wall hangings, and higher-status chamber storage |
| Holy Roman Empire / German | c. 1200AD | German-speaking imperial regions and neighbouring central European towns | heavy oak storage, strong chests, wardrobes, iron locks, tiled or metal heating objects, shelves, benches, and urban household wares |
| Christian Iberian | c. 1200AD | Leonese, Castilian, Aragonese, Navarrese, and neighbouring Christian Iberian domestic practice | olivewood and oak storage, ceramic vessels, low chests, iron keys, domestic textiles, and mixed western-Islamic household forms |
| Andalusian | c. 1100AD | al-Andalus and western Islamic Iberia | cedar storage, low tables, ceramic and glazed wares, scent and spice containers, lamps, curtains, carved boxes, and textile-rich interiors |
| Byzantine | c. 1000AD | middle Byzantine Constantinopolitan and provincial court-facing households | display shelves, walnut storage, bronze lamps, fine boxes, ceremonial vessels, hangings, locks, and mixed practical and courtly domestic wares |
| Abbasid | c. 850AD | Baghdad-centred early medieval Islamic court and urban households | cedar document cabinets, low storage, lamps, scent containers, domestic writing goods, ceramic vessels, cushions, and textile-rich room dividers |
| Fatimid | c. 1050AD | Fatimid Egypt and North African textile culture | linen cupboards, light warm-climate storage, ceramic wares, oil lamps, textile hangings, scent containers, and portable household goods |
| Seljuk / Ayyubid-Mamluk | c. 1200AD | Anatolia, Syria, Egypt, and crusading-era Islamic eastern Mediterranean | tooled chests, cedar storage, low furniture, metal lamps, woven containers, ceramic vessels, warm textiles, and urban household wares |
| Magyar | c. 950AD | Carpathian Basin Magyar households with steppe and central European influences | portable travel chests, leather pouches, bronze-mounted storage, compact furniture, packs, timber gates, and iron locks |
| Rus / Novgorod | c. 1100AD | Kievan Rus and Novgorod-facing northern Slavic/Norse-Byzantine intersections | pine and birch storage, winter chests, heavy shelves, wool and fur containers, iron latches, plank doors, and sturdy benches |
| Steppe Turkic / Mongol | c. 1200AD | Inner Eurasian mounted steppe households before and during early imperial Mongol expansion | felt-lined chests, travel coffers, saddlebag-like storage, portable packs, folding or low furniture, leather containers, and robust domestic wares |
| North Indian / Rajput | c. 1100AD | north and north-western Indian households before heavy late Sultanate/Mughal tailoring dominance | teak chests, cotton bags, brass and ceramic wares, low furniture, carved boxes, domestic lamps, and warm-climate storage |
| South Indian / Chola | c. 1050AD | Chola and neighbouring south Indian temple and court household environment | teak and sandalwood boxes, bamboo hampers, brass vessels, oil lamps, low seats, cotton storage, and decorative domestic wares |
| Song China | c. 1100AD | Northern and Southern Song domestic forms useful for a broad East Asian baseline | cypress scroll cabinets, bamboo shelves, low tables, paper and brush storage, ceramic vessels, lanterns, and restrained display storage |
| Goryeo Korea | c. 1150AD | Goryeo-period Korean household and court-facing domestic practice | pine clothing chests, silk-lined personal containers, ceramic vessels, low storage, lamps, cushions, and practical wooden furniture |
| Heian / Kamakura Japan | c. 1200AD | late Heian to early Kamakura domestic and court-facing practice | cypress boxes, bamboo paper shelves, low desks, floor cushions, ceramic wares, lamps, and compact storage for court or warrior households |

### Religious service coverage table

| Service family | Coverage focus |
|---|---|
| Shared religious | Cross-tradition offering tables, shrine shelves, lecterns, display cases, reliquary boxes, alms chests, incense equipment, ritual water vessels, oil lamps, candles, and supply cupboards |
| Latin Christian | Church pews, fonts, holy-water stoups, thuribles, wall crucifixes, rood-screen panels, altar tables, lecterns, reliquary containers, tabernacle storage, sacristy cupboards, offertory chests, and funeral furnishings |
| Eastern Christian | Iconostasis panels, icon stands, vigil lamps, analogia, service tables, reliquary cases, incense equipment, baptismal basins, and chanting benches |
| Islamic | Mosque and madrasa furnishings including minbar and mihrab forms, Qur'an storage, book stands, mosque lamps, ablution vessels, charity boxes, sandal shelves, and incense burners |
| Jewish | Torah ark and scroll storage, bimah tables, reading lecterns, synagogue lamps, washing vessels, charity boxes, genizah chests, memorial candles, benches, and wall panels |
| Hindu | Temple and shrine altar furniture, puja shelves, offering trays, oil lamps, incense burners, ritual water vessels, image pedestals, garland racks, donation storage, and shrine screens |
| Buddhist | Altar tables, sutra cabinets and chests, reading lecterns, incense burners, offering vessels, lamps, reliquary boxes, meditation benches, memorial shelves, and alms chests |
| Daoist | Altar tables, scripture cabinets, talisman-paper chests, incense urns, offering trays, ritual ewers, altar lamps, tablet shelves, donation boxes, and altar screens |
| Shinto | Kamidana shelves, offering stands, purification basins, donation boxes, votive tablet racks, shrine lamps, and pale shrine screens |
| Northern and ancestral | Sacred posts, offering bowls and chests, ancestor shelves, libation bowls, and sacred-hall benches |

## Seeder and project grounding rules

- Use the project-standard `CreateItem(...)` seeder call shape for implementation output.
- `uniqueReference` values use lowercase snake case ASCII and should remain stable once accepted.
- `noun` is compact and singular, usually the closest ordinary object name.
- `sdesc` is player-facing, concise, normally begins with `a`, `an`, or `a pair of`, and should not end with a full stop.
- `ldesc` should normally be `null` for this catalogue because the target items are moveable prototypes.
- `fdesc` should be player-facing in-world prose: shape, visible material, construction, finish, wear, markings, fittings, and obvious affordances.
- `material` must be an exact seeded solid material. Liquids and gases are not substitutes for solid primary materials.
- `tags` must be exact seeded hierarchical tag paths.
- `components` must be exact seeded component prototype names.
- `inherentCost` is denominated in farthings. Use whole-farthing values unless a fractional farthing is deliberately intended.
- No target row should use a morph target, morph emote, morph timer, hidden-player flag, or non-skinnable flag by default.

## Component and portability rules

The catalogue is authored as a moveable prototype catalogue. Every catalogue row therefore includes `Holdable`, including bulky furniture and doors. Installed copies can be created as separate fixture variants with room long descriptions and without `Holdable` where the implementation requires that distinction.

Every catalogue row uses exactly one destroyable component. Furniture, shelves, cupboards, chests, doors, and heavy wooden goods normally use `Destroyable_Furniture`, `Destroyable_WoodenHeavy`, or `Destroyable_Door`; small textile, leather, and ordinary domestic goods normally use `Destroyable_Misc`; heavy metal safes and lockboxes use `Destroyable_HeavyMetal`; fragile glassware uses `Destroyable_Glassware`; paper packets use `Destroyable_Paper`.

Do not stack components that provide the same exclusive interface. In practice, this means each dry container row should use exactly one `Container_*` or `LockingContainer_*` component, each liquid vessel should use exactly one `LContainer_*` component, and an item should not combine dry-container and liquid-container behaviour unless a later engine-specific design explicitly supports separate compartments.

## Component implementation rules for this catalogue

### Containers

- Use `Container_Pouch`, `Container_Purse`, `Container_Sachet`, `Container_Sack`, `Container_Pack`, `Container_Tote`, `Container_Quiver`, `Container_Open_Bin`, `Container_Footlocker`, `Container_Trunk`, `Container_Blanket_Box`, `Container_Cupboard`, `Container_Small_Cabinet`, `Container_Large_Cabinet`, `Container_Wide_Shelves`, `Container_Narrow_Shelves`, `Container_Wall_Shelf`, `Container_Bookcase_Shelves`, `Container_Display_Shelves`, `Container_Dresser`, `Container_Wardrobe`, `Container_Armoire`, `Container_Desk_Drawers`, `Container_Hutch`, `Container_Sideboard`, `Container_Nightstand`, `Container_Side_Table`, `Container_Table`, `Container_Small_Table`, `Container_Large_Table`, `Container_Counter`, `Container_Bed_Surface`, `Container_Cot_Surface`, `Container_Bench_Surface`, and `Container_Couch_Surface` for household dry storage and surface-storage behaviour.
- Use `Container_Open_Bin` for open baskets, hampers, bins, and similar open household containers.
- Use `Container_Wide_Shelves`, `Container_Narrow_Shelves`, `Container_Wall_Shelf`, `Container_Bookcase_Shelves`, and `Container_Display_Shelves` for shelf-like storage that should hold things on it rather than inside it.
- Use `Container_Pack`, `Container_Tote`, `Container_Pouch`, and `Container_Purse` with wearable or beltable companions when the item is meant to be carried on the body.

### Locking containers

- Use `LockingContainer_Lockbox` for small built-in-lock caskets, cash boxes, jewel boxes, document boxes, charity boxes, seal boxes, and compact high-value storage.
- Use `LockingContainer_Footlocker` for larger built-in-lock chests, coffers, strongboxes, scripture chests, dowry chests, merchant chests, and ordinary lockable household or institutional storage.
- Use `LockingContainer_SafeChest` for deliberately heavy safe-chest objects such as treasury chests, guild strong chests, household strong chests, and protected scroll or charter storage where the object should read as exceptional.
- Built-in locking containers should not also receive loose `Warded_Lock_*`, `Latch_*`, `Keyring_*`, ordinary `Container_*`, `Door_*`, or `Gate_*` components.
- Ordinary chests, cupboards, trunks, and shelves that can later be secured by an affixed external lock should use their normal `Container_*` component and should not be described as having working built-in lock behaviour.
- Locking-container public descriptions can show hasps, plates, keyholes, strapping, hinges, lids, panels, and reinforced corners, but should not promise key compatibility or picking difficulty beyond the seeded component profile.

### Wearable and beltable containers

- Use `Beltable` for belt pouches, belt purses, belt waterskins, belt quivers, and small cases that attach to a belt rather than being worn independently.
- Use `Wear_Backpack` with `Container_Pack` for back-worn packs and basket backpacks.
- Use `Wear_Shoulder` with `Container_Tote`, `Container_Quiver`, or `LContainer_Waterskin` for shoulder-carried bags, satchels, quivers, and waterskins.
- Use `Wear_Waist` sparingly for waist-worn purses or broad waist containers that are worn directly rather than merely clipped to another belt item.

### Liquid containers and liquid sources

- Use `LContainer_Flask`, `LContainer_Waterskin`, `LContainer_Canteen`, bottle profiles, gallon-cask profiles, and amphora profiles for flasks, cruets, waterskins, canteens, bottles, small casks, jars, amphorae, urns, and household water-storage vessels.
- Liquid containers already provide liquid-container, openable, and lockable interfaces. Do not stack dry `Container_*`, `LockingContainer_*`, `WaterSource`, `Lantern`, or other liquid-container behaviours on the same row.
- Ordinary liquid vessels are finite containers: they can hold liquid but do not refill themselves. Self-refilling or site-supplied water objects belong to the water-source category.
- Material remains the solid dominant vessel material, not the contained liquid. Use `earthenware`, `stoneware`, `terracotta`, `ceramic`, `wood`, `oak`, `leather`, `glass`, `bronze`, `brass`, or another exact seeded solid material as appropriate.

### Water sources and washing fixtures

- Water-source rows use exactly one `WaterSource` component, plus `Holdable` and one destroyable component. `WaterSource` components are self-replenishing liquid containers and therefore should not be combined with ordinary `LContainer_*`, dry `Container_*`, `LockingContainer_*`, `Lantern`, or other liquid-container behaviours.
- Use `Sink_5L`, `Sink_20L`, and `Sink_50L` for fillable basins, sinks, wash tubs, scullery sinks, laundry tubs, and troughs that behave like finite fillable fixtures.
- Use `Bathtub` for large bath tubs and bathing basins.
- Use `Infinite_PublicCisternWaterSource`, `Infinite_PublicPumpWaterSource`, `Infinite_PublicTroughWaterSource`, `Infinite_WaterSource`, `Infinite_SpringWaterSource`, `Infinite_RiverWaterSource`, `Infinite_LakeWaterSource`, `Infinite_BrackishWaterSource`, `Infinite_SaltWaterSource`, and `Infinite_SwampWaterSource` for self-refilling public, household, or environmental water sources.
- `WaterSource_ProgControlled` is reserved for water sources whose supply should be controlled by local script or builder logic.
- Modern-leaning source profiles such as `Infinite_PublicTapWaterSource`, `Infinite_StandpipeWaterSource`, `Infinite_DrinkingFountainWaterSource`, `WaterSource_Canteen`, and `WaterSource_DisposableBottle` are available in the seeded catalogue but are not default medieval household prototypes.
- All rows in this reference remain portable/installable prototypes and therefore include `Holdable`. Later room-fixture variants may deliberately omit `Holdable` if the item should become permanent infrastructure.

### Food service, tableware, and household vessels

- Open eating and serving wares such as plates, trenchers, bowls for thick foods, dry condiment dishes, platters, boards, and trays use exactly one dry container component: `Container_Plate` for plate-like or bowl-like individual/open vessels and `Container_Tray` for trays, platters, boards, and broad serving surfaces.
- Drinking, pouring, washing, and liquid-storage wares use exactly one liquid-container component and should not also receive `Container_Plate` or `Container_Tray`.
- Bowls and basins are assigned by functional behaviour, not by name alone: a porringer or grain bowl that only needs to hold solid or thick food can use `Container_Plate`; a wash basin, water basin, wine-mixing bowl, or water jar should use a liquid-container profile.
- Tableware rows are generally `VerySmall`, `Small`, or `Normal`; large water jars, laundry basins, feast boards, and amphora-like vessels may be `Large` when their empty form is bulky.
- Domestic tableware remains non-religious unless explicitly classified in the religious-furnishings category. Religious altar vessels, fonts, censers, reliquaries, offering bowls, and sacred lamps stay out of this category.
- Edible trenchers are listed as plate-like eating surfaces in this catalogue. A later implementation should give the edible trencher an edible component or dedicated edible-container hybrid if the engine supports that combination cleanly.

### Doors, gates, grates, and doorway hangings

- Use `Door_*` for ordinary opaque doors without built-in lock behaviour.
- Use `Door_Lockable_*` for opaque doors with built-in lock behaviour.
- Use `Gate_*` for gate-like, barred, grille-like, lattice, or grate objects. Seeded gate components are door components whose behaviour allows sight and ranged fire through the barrier.
- Use `Gate_Lockable_*` for gate-like, barred, grille-like, lattice, or grate objects with built-in lock behaviour.
- Use weak ordinary door profiles such as `Door_Bad_Large` or `Door_Bad_VeryLarge` for soft doorway hangings, furs, bead hangings, carpets, curtains, flaps, and screen-like doorway barriers when they need door-like open/close behaviour.
- Size `Large` is the normal household-door size, `VeryLarge` is the normal yard or compound gate size, and `Huge` is reserved for town-gate leaves, major grates, portcullis-like barriers, and very large compound entrances.
- Built-in lockable doors and gates should not also receive loose `Warded_Lock_*`, `Latch_*`, `Keyring_*`, or `LockingContainer_*` components. Doors intended to accept an affixed lock later use ordinary non-lockable `Door_*` or `Gate_*` components.

### Locks, latches, keys, and lock hardware

- Loose mechanical locks use `Warded_Lock_*` components. Loose keys use `Warded_Key`. Keyrings use `Keyring_Small` or `Keyring_Large`, not ordinary container components.
- One-sided latches use exactly one `Latch_*` component. Latches are closure hardware with lock-like behaviour and should not be stacked with loose `Lock`, built-in `LockingContainer_*`, built-in `Door_Lockable_*`, or built-in `Gate_Lockable_*` behaviours.
- Use `Latch_Container_Hasp` for chest, trunk, coffer, casket, and lockable-container hasp latches.
- Use `Latch_Container_Hook` for hook-and-eye latches on cupboards, small hatches, shutters, lids, and light compartment doors.
- Use `Latch_Door_Bar` for one-sided sliding bars, door bars, drawbolts, shop shutters, stable doors, and interior shutters.
- Use `Latch_Gate_DropBar` for yard gates, stable gates, palisade gates, courtyard gates, and similar heavy gate closures.
- Use `Latch_Portcullis_Pawl` for portcullis pawls, winch pawls, brake latches, and heavy raised-barrier mechanisms.
- Use simple quality latches such as `Latch_Terrible`, `Latch_Bad`, `Latch_Normal`, `Latch_Good`, and `Latch_Excellent` when the object is a generic one-sided latch rather than a specific hasp, hook, bar, gate, or pawl form.
- Passive lock hardware such as staples, escutcheons, strike plates, straps, and bar brackets can remain inert portable hardware with `Holdable` and one destroyable component only. These rows should not claim to lock, unlock, latch, or hold keys unless a seeded behaviour component is present.
- Built-in locking containers use `LockingContainer_*`; built-in lockable doors and gates use `Door_Lockable_*` or `Gate_Lockable_*`. Do not combine those built-in behaviours with loose lock, latch, or keyring components on the same item.
- Modern or electronic lock concepts, keypads, maglocks, automation housings, and locksmithing toolkits are outside this medieval household hardware slice.

### Furniture and furniture surfaces

- Tables use a seeded `Table_*` component when they should support table seating/occupant behaviour. Pair the table component with one appropriate surface container such as `Container_Small_Table`, `Container_Side_Table`, `Container_Table`, `Container_Large_Table`, or `Container_Desk_Surface` when the surface should hold placed items.
- Benches use `Bench_Double` or `Bench_Triple`; do not stack a `Bench_*` component with a separate `Table_*` component because bench components already provide table-style occupancy behaviour. Add `Container_Bench_Surface` when the bench should also support placed items.
- Chairs, stools, thrones, high seats, floor cushions, and floor mats use `Chair_Single`, `Chair_Double`, `Chair_Triple`, or `Chair_Quad` according to intended seating capacity. Soft cushions and mats normally use `Destroyable_Misc`; wooden seats normally use `Destroyable_Furniture`.
- Beds, cots, bedrolls, pallets, cradles, and sleeping platforms use `Container_Bed_Surface` or `Container_Cot_Surface` because the current seeded catalogue provides surface behaviour rather than a dedicated sleep/bed component. Descriptions should present them as sleeping surfaces without promising mechanics beyond the seeded component.
- Desks, writing tables, writing boards, reading stands, account tables, and copyist surfaces use `Container_Desk_Surface`; use a `Table_*` component as well only when the item should behave as a table with occupant positions. Drawer or cabinet storage belongs to the domestic furniture-container category.
- Furniture rows should not use loose lock, latch, key, keyring, door, gate, wearable, or beltable components unless a future category explicitly defines that hybrid.

### Lighting and heating

- Use `Candle`, `Candle_Bright`, and `Candle_Long` for wax candles, tapers, rushlights, candle-lanterns, and other small wick lights whose behaviour is candle-like.
- Use `Torch_1Hour`, `Torch_2Hour`, `Torch_3Hour`, and `SignalFire` for brands, torches, cresset flames, warning baskets, and other open-flame objects whose behaviour is torch-like.
- Use `Lantern` for oil lamps and shielded fuel-burning lanterns. Do not add separate `LContainer_*` or dry `Container_*` components to these rows because `Lantern` already supplies liquid-fuel, openable/lockable, lightable, and light-producing behaviour.
- Use `SolidFuelHeaterCooler_Brazier` for braziers, fire bowls, ember pans, warming pans, fire baskets, and charcoal heaters where solid-fuel heat is the primary mechanical behaviour. Do not add a separate `Container_*` component to brazier rows.
- Candle trays, sconces, torch brackets, lamp stands, lantern hooks, lamp shelves, and candle racks are support furnishings. They may use surface/storage components such as `Container_Tray`, `Container_Wall_Shelf`, `Container_Small_Table`, or `Container_Wide_Shelves`, but should not claim to produce light unless they carry an actual light-producing component.
- Religiously explicit lamps, censers, sanctuary lights, and sacred lampstands belong in the religious-furnishings category. Cooking hearths, ovens, cauldrons, pans, spits, and kitchen cookware belong outside this lighting slice.

### Decorative household wares

- Decorative household wares are portable, non-religious household decoration: carpets, rugs, mats, hangings, curtains, bed dressings, table dressings, screens, panels, mirrors, plaques, and similar room ornaments.
- Decorative rows are intentionally inert unless a future category assigns a specific behaviour. In this catalogue they use `Holdable` and one destroyable component only, with no door, gate, dry-container, liquid-container, seating, table, lock, key, wearable, beltable, light-source, or heat-source behaviour.
- Descriptions may say that an item is laid, hung, leaned, suspended, draped, folded, or used as a divider, but should not claim that it blocks movement, blocks sight, provides cover, acts as a door, seats occupants, stores contents, or produces light without a matching component.
- Doorway curtains, bead hangings, furs, and carpet flaps with open/close behaviour belong in the door-hanging category. Religious display objects belong in the religious-furnishings category. Functional storage cloths, table surfaces, sleeping surfaces, and seating objects belong in their own functional categories.
- Skins should carry local motifs, dye patterns, embroidery, household symbols, story scenes, paint style, carving pattern, textile border, and display status without changing the inert decorative behaviour.

### Religious containers and furnishings

- Religious furnishings remain portable seeded prototypes in this reference and therefore include `Holdable`, even when their real-world use would often be fixed in a church, mosque, synagogue, temple, shrine, chapel, or sacred hall.
- Religious dry containers use one appropriate `Container_*` or `LockingContainer_*` component. Use ordinary `Container_*` components for open shelves, cupboards, display cases, trays, offering bowls, and scripture surfaces. Use `LockingContainer_*` components only when the prototype has a built-in lock, such as alms chests, donation boxes, reliquary boxes, Torah arks, scripture chests, and temple store boxes.
- Sacred-water, ablution, libation, oil, and washing vessels use one `LContainer_*` component or a component that already implements liquid-container behaviour. Do not combine these with dry `Container_*` components.
- Religious lamps, candles, and censers use light or heat components directly: `Candle`, `Candle_Long`, `Lantern`, or `SolidFuelHeaterCooler_Brazier`. A candle rack, lamp tray, or lampstand that is only a support surface should not claim to produce light unless it has an actual light-producing component.
- Religious screens, panels, crucifixes, icon panels, mihrab panels, pedestals, and sacred posts can legitimately be inert portable furnishings with only `Holdable` and one destroyable component when no functional component matches the intended behaviour.
- This category does not include religious outfits, vestments, wearable devotional pieces, jewellery-like objects, amulets, charms, prayer beads, or small personal religious props.

## Tags, materials, sizes, quality, and cost assumptions

- Dominant materials in the household catalogue include exact seeded materials such as `oak`, `pine`, `beech`, `birch`, `walnut`, `cedar`, `cypress`, `teak`, `bamboo`, `sandalwood`, `olive wood`, `willow`, `linen`, `wool`, `cotton`, `hemp`, `jute`, `canvas`, `burlap`, `felt`, `silk`, `leather`, `animal skin`, `fur`, `paper`, `parchment`, `earthenware`, `stoneware`, `terracotta`, `ceramic`, `porcelain`, `glass`, `bronze`, `brass`, `wrought iron`, `cast iron`, `bone`, `horn`, and `ivory`.
- Size targets: `Tiny` and `VerySmall` for packets, sachets, purses, and small pouches; `Small` for satchels, lockboxes, waterskins, quivers, small cases, and small boxes; `Normal` for packs, hampers, baskets, nightstands, and compact furniture; `Large` for cupboards, shelves, chests, wardrobes, doors, and storage furniture; `VeryLarge` and `Huge` for larger gates and barriers.
- Quality targets: `Standard` for ordinary household goods; `Good` for carved, fine, elite, lockable, metal-fitted, high-status, or more labour-intensive goods. Extreme quality levels are not used by default.
- Use broad exact seeded tags, especially `Functions / Container`, `Functions / Container / Open Container`, `Functions / Container / Porous Container`, `Functions / Container / Watertight Container`, `Functions / Household Items / Household Furniture`, `Functions / Household Items / Household Wares`, `Functions / Household Items / Household Decorations`, `Functions / Household Items / Household Lighting`, `Functions / Household Items / Household Heating`, and relevant market tags under `Market / Household Goods` or `Market / Lighting`. Water-source rows generally also use `Functions / Container / Watertight Container` because the source component behaves as a liquid container.

## Skin strategy

- All target household items should be skinnable because they are finished goods or manufactured domestic objects.
- Skins should carry high-variance presentation: household marks, clan marks, heraldic devices, auspicious motifs, local religious designs, carving patterns, painted colours, weaving patterns, textile borders, maker marks, lock ornamentation, key-bow shape, vessel glaze, and elite-status finish.
- Skins should not change component behaviour. A skinned dry box remains a dry container; a skinned liquid vessel remains a liquid container; a skinned ordinary door does not become a built-in-lock door.

| Locks, latches, keys, and lock hardware | 87 |

## Item catalogue targets

### Household and trade containers

This category covers dry trade, industry, and mercantile storage such as sacks, bales, bundles, crates, bins, trays, boxes, lockboxes, chests, dry barrels, jars, and canisters. Liquid barrels, jugs, waterskins, and drinking vessels belong to liquid-container and vessel categories.

### Domestic personal containers

This category covers personal domestic storage such as pouches, purses, wallets, sachets, satchels, shoulder bags, packs, bundles, waterskins, quivers, document cases, sewing boxes, and other portable household containers.

### Domestic furniture containers

### Religious containers and furnishings

This category covers communal religious containers, furnishings, display objects, offering storage, scripture storage, sanctuary furniture, sacred-water vessels, lamps, incense equipment, and institutional religious storage. It excludes religious clothing, jewellery-like personal devotional objects, and small personal props.

### Food service, tableware, and household vessels

This category covers plates, trenchers, chargers, dishes, bowls, porringers, platters, trays, cups, beakers, mugs, drinking horns, goblets, jugs, pitchers, ewers, flagons, cruets, decanters, flasks, jars, amphora-like vessels, wash basins, rinse bowls, household water jars, and pouring vessels.

### Water sources and washing fixtures

This category covers portable/installable water-source prototypes and fillable washing fixtures: basins, sinks, wash tubs, troughs, bath tubs, wellheads, cisterns, pumps, spring boxes, river draw points, lake draw points, saltwater and brackish sources, and controlled water basins. Ordinary jugs, ewers, waterskins, and barrels remain in vessel or container categories.

### Doors, gates, door-hangings, and grates

This category covers ordinary and lockable doors, gates, grates, portcullis-like barriers, doorway curtains, furs, bead hangings, carpet flaps, and other movable door-like objects sized for domestic, public, service, yard, and fortified thresholds.

### Locks, latches, keys, and lock hardware

This category covers loose locks, one-sided latches, keys, keyrings, hasps, staples, lock plates, strike plates, escutcheons, bar brackets, gate latches, and other portable lock hardware. Built-in locking doors and built-in locking containers remain in their own functional categories.

### Lighting and heating

This category covers candles, tapers, rushlight-style lights, torches, brands, watch lights, signal fire baskets, cressets, oil lamps, lanterns, braziers, fire bowls, ember pans, warming pans, fire baskets, lamp stands, candle stands, sconces, trays, hooks, brackets, and lamp racks.

### Decorative household wares

This category covers inert, non-religious household decoration such as carpets, rugs, mats, runners, wall hangings, tapestries, painted cloths, banners, wall curtains, room-divider hangings, canopy cloths, window hangings, valances, table covers, bench covers, bed coverlets, bed curtains, screens, panels, mirrors, and plaques.
